<div id="review_1740701832" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1740701832" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1740701832?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781780746357" href="/book/show/25694095-a-brief-history-of-seven-killings">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=jamaica">jamaica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1740701832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11523093863134097505"><i>"But a man can only move so far before leash pull him back. Before the master say, Enough of that shit, that's not where we going. The leash of Babylon, the leash of the police code, the leash of Gun Court, the leash of twenty-three families that run Jamaica."</i> <br/><br/>Marlon James finds highly evocative ways to poetically untangle recent Jamaican history - this book is fictional, but it talks about real events. Putting the attempted assasination of Bob Marley right before a planned peace concert. At the</span>
  <span id="freeText11523093863134097505" style="display:none"><i>"But a man can only move so far before leash pull him back. Before the master say, Enough of that shit, that's not where we going. The leash of Babylon, the leash of the police code, the leash of Gun Court, the leash of twenty-three families that run Jamaica."</i> <br /><br />Marlon James finds highly evocative ways to poetically untangle recent Jamaican history - this book is fictional, but it talks about real events. Putting the attempted assasination of Bob Marley right before a planned peace concert. At the narrative center, James creates a whole tornado of characters and language in order to display who had an interest in preventing peace and why, and what the consequences of these dynamics were. <br /><br />In the 70's, Kingston has been dominated by gangs that were connected to political parties, the Jamaica Labour Party (JLP) and the People's National Party (PNP). The gangs had built a whole system of repression and benefits to secure their respective rule, especially in their territories in Western Kingston:<br /><br /><i>"That's why neither the JLP nor the PNP fucking with the peace treaty. Peace can't happen when to much to gain at war. And who want peace anyway when all that mean is that you still poor?"</i><br /><br />Not only was the CIA involved in Jamaican politics at the time, the two gangs also had one common enemy: The corrupt police:<br /><br /><i>"Babylon out to kill you whether you was an animal with stripes or spots."</i><br /><br />And anyway, who can guarantee the people of Kingston that the police force will be able to provide a better order than the one created by the gangs?<br /><br /><i>"The second you say peace this and peace that, and let's talk about peace, is the second gunman put down their guns. But guess what, white boy. As soon as you put down your gun the policeman pull out his gun. Dangerous thing, peace."</i><br /><br />Told in five parts with several chapters each, the story covers the 70's, 80's (during the crack wars), and 90's. The cast of characters as listed at the beginning of the novel contains 76 people, one of them being a ghost. The point of view changes in every chapter and, God help us all, large parts are told in Jamaican patois, some as a stream-of-consciousness, and one even in free-flowing verse. There is murder, violence, torture, fear, poverty, all kinds of drugs, corruption, straight and gay sex.<br /><br />In the later parts, the story moves from Jamaica to the United States, where Jamaican crews connected to the Kingston gangs run a drug trade - it's the same war, just fought differently.<br /><br />I just love James' wild imagination, how masterfully he manages to hold this wide-ranging story together, the cinematic descriptions, the well-drawn characters, and the way he employs narrative techniques. I wish he had taught at Macalester earlier, so I could have taken a class with him. <br /><br />A well-deserved Booker win for Marlon James.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11523093863134097505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1416175671" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1416175671" />
    <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph"><img alt="Paul E. Morph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602970987p2/44824883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1416175671?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul E. Morph" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph">Paul E. Morph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/24646670-a-brief-history-of-seven-killings">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44824883-paul-e-morph?shelf=books-read-in-2017">books-read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1416175671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12143820414012936236">Marlon James' third novel, <i>A Brief History of Seven Killings</i>, is set in a Jamaica that lies exposed like a dead badger at the roadside with all its glistening innards hanging out, being picked at by carrion birds. I mean this in a good way, you understand. James writes like he's painting the Sistine Chapel with words. I've never been to Jamaica but after reading this book the place lives and breathes inside my mind nonetheless. When the focus shifts to the US in the second half of the book, thou</span>
  <span id="freeText12143820414012936236" style="display:none">Marlon James' third novel, <i>A Brief History of Seven Killings</i>, is set in a Jamaica that lies exposed like a dead badger at the roadside with all its glistening innards hanging out, being picked at by carrion birds. I mean this in a good way, you understand. James writes like he's painting the Sistine Chapel with words. I've never been to Jamaica but after reading this book the place lives and breathes inside my mind nonetheless. When the focus shifts to the US in the second half of the book, though, James doesn't quite bring it to life quite as well, which is a shame. Maybe it's because I've been to the States; I don't know.<br /><br />The author populates his beautifully painted landscape with a multitude of different characters, some living and some ghostly, all of whom are fleshed out, believable and speak with unique voices... and it felt like most of them took a turn at bat, narration-wise. I still feel like I have a distinctly un-heavenly host of shades living in my skull.<br /><br />The plot is just as wide-ranging as the cast. It encompasses multiple continents and many decades. It's anything but brief and includes an awful lot more than seven killings. (This has been said by virtually every reviewer of this book but, Hell, I never claimed to be original.) It inevitably contains almost everything life has to offer and then some. <br /><br />To be honest, I was completely swept up by this book and carried along a very long, rocky road by a very skilled driver. The reason I haven't given this great book five stars is that it's such an exhaustive, comprehensive, far-reaching complexity of settings, characters and events that it very nearly collapses under its own weight at times. Also, when all is said and done, I wasn't entirely certain what the author was actually trying to say... I mean, drugs and murder and crime and corruption are bad, mmmKay, is a valid message that bears repeating but... it's also kind of obvious to anyone with at least half a brain. Did I miss some deeper message? Hell, it's certainly possible.<br /><br />Please don't let my (minor) criticisms put you off reading this book though; it really is something quite special and I'll definitely be reading more by this author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12143820414012936236" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1614039978" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1614039978" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1614039978?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781594633942" href="/book/show/24611846-a-brief-history-of-seven-killings">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=south-america">south-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=jamaica">jamaica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1614039978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8629925203345660411"><b>Can you dig it, dingledoodies?</b><br/><br/><a href='http://s1178.photobucket.com/user/kemoll/media/marley4_2285436k.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463390660i/19112185.jpg' alt=' photo marley4_2285436k.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Futile to say that this is not for the faint of heart, as they would never get past the four page cast of characters, past the opening chapter written by a dead man, his head a smashed pumpkin and two arms bent in a way that arms aren't supposed to bend, past the screams and the blood and the vomit, the dirt rising in a flood then back down like a reflux, past the first chapter in Bam-Bam's voice, gut-wrenching, harrowing: you want change? We will have to wait a</span>
  <span id="freeText8629925203345660411" style="display:none"><b>Can you dig it, dingledoodies?</b><br><br><a href="http://s1178.photobucket.com/user/kemoll/media/marley4_2285436k.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463390660i/19112185.jpg" alt=" photo marley4_2285436k.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Futile to say that this is not for the faint of heart, as they would never get past the four page cast of characters, past the opening chapter written by a dead man, his head a smashed pumpkin and two arms bent in a way that arms aren't supposed to bend, past the screams and the blood and the vomit, the dirt rising in a flood then back down like a reflux, past the first chapter in Bam-Bam's voice, gut-wrenching, harrowing: you want change? We will have to wait and see, <i>but all we can do down here in the Eight Lanes is see and wait...<blockquote>and the boys like me not in school very regular and can't read Dick and Jane but know Coca-Cola, and want to go to a studio and cut a tune and sing hit songs and ride the riddim out of the ghetto but Copenhagen City and the Eight Lanes both too big and every time you reach the edge, the edge move ahead of you like a shadow until the whole world is a ghetto, and you wait.</blockquote></i>.<br><br><b>Seven</b> killings? More surely, I gave up counting. <i>Killing don't need no reason. This is ghetto. Reason is for rich people. We have madness.</i><br><br>Madness, this kind of untamed narrative energy. Wild, demanding, bold and vibrant with guts and greasy slicks of blood. Madness - not crazy people madness, but angry madness, hungry madness, bitter bile in the throat madness, CIA madness, gun madness. <br><br>And you read, and every time you reach the edge, the edge move ahead of you like a shadow.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="8629925203345660411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1392826297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1392826297" />
    <a title="Britta Böhler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38530619-britta-b-hler"><img alt="Britta Böhler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546399505p2/38530619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1392826297?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Britta Böhler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Britta Böhler" href="/user/show/38530619-britta-b-hler">Britta Böhler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38530619-britta-b-hler?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1392826297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12164325397932365540">How to talk intelligently about this novel? And how to feel about it?<br/><br/>The book is a tour de force, and no, not because of its length. It's dense, packed with information, and the scope is enormous. The historic setting doesn't function as an anchor, like it does in other 'big reads', at least if you are not familiar with Jamaican history in the 1970ies- 1990ies. The variety of characters, the language, and the - at times - very graphic violence don't make it an easy read either. But hey, nobody s</span>
  <span id="freeText12164325397932365540" style="display:none">How to talk intelligently about this novel? And how to feel about it?<br /><br />The book is a tour de force, and no, not because of its length. It's dense, packed with information, and the scope is enormous. The historic setting doesn't function as an anchor, like it does in other 'big reads', at least if you are not familiar with Jamaican history in the 1970ies- 1990ies. The variety of characters, the language, and the - at times - very graphic violence don't make it an easy read either. But hey, nobody said literature had to be easy. Or fun.<br /><br />This is Dickens on speed but without the lengthy descriptions that allow the reader to take a break and rest his or her mind for a bit. <br /><br />Most difficult, though, is to feel attachment to some of the characters. Not only because there are so many but mainly because even in 700 pages there is not enough room to give all the characters the depth they deserve. This is especially true - at least it was for me - with regard to the new characters introduced in the second half of the book. I found it hard to let the, by then, familiar characters go and get acquainted with a whole new set of players. Not surprising, then, that most readers (including me) find Nina one of the most engaging characters of the novel. We stay with her the longest, throughout the whole book and we get to know her the best.<br /><br />But don't get me wrong: there is much beauty in the book, and, despite the violence, plenty of humor. The chapters featuring Barry Diflorio (the last name already!), CIA station chief in Jamaica, are at times hilariously funny and I was sad to see him being swept off to Argentina... And the politician, Arthur Jennings, now deceased, 'ghosting' around in between the chapters works brilliantly.<br /><br />Maybe this is a book you can only fully appreciate, if you read it more than once. No, strike the maybe, that's exactly what you have to do. I am just not sure I will...<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12164325397932365540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Qn7/magvmbhqnrgd/TSlCZmSGWwGVB+Ua0E4ybtkBa+FhcYDUs076Y8Wy/sIVDDMdFjabs2yF54WkyeNqlz3wg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('doTyCO4+I60TVwFMHL+dWMIPB1f9s7/XKZI2lDX5APGxf8uSFNyB/Pbfcqrp3widL8XEVTZVt91UQCnQJMHynA==')})
  }

It challenges the sacred tenets of the United States Constitution, urban life, the civil rights movement, the father-son relationship, and the holy grail of racial equality―the black Chinese restaurant.\n\nBorn in the &quot;agrarian ghetto&quot; of Dickens―on the southern outskirts of Los Angeles―the narrator of The Sellout resigns himself to the fate of lower-middle-class Californians: &quot;I\'d die in the same bedroom I\'d grown up in, looking up at the cracks in the stucco ceiling that\'ve been there since \'68 quake.&quot; Raised by a single father, a controversial sociologist, he spent his childhood as the subject in racially charged psychological studies. He is led to believe that his father\'s pioneering work will result in a memoir that will solve his family\'s financial woes. But when his father is killed in a police shoot-out, he realizes there never was a memoir. All that\'s left is the bill for a drive-thru funeral.\n\nFueled by this deceit and the general disrepair of his hometown, the narrator sets out to right another wrong: Dickens has literally been removed from the map to save California from further embarrassment. Enlisting the help of the town\'s most famous resident―the last surviving Little Rascal, Hominy Jenkins―he initiates the most outrageous action conceivable: reinstating slavery and segregating the Kidane, an officer in Emperor Haile Selassie’s army, rushes to mobilize his strongest men before the Italians invade. His initial kindness to Hirut shifts into a flinty cruelty when she resists his advances, and Hirut finds herself tumbling into a new world of thefts and violations, of betrayals and overwhelming rage. Meanwhile, Mussolini’s technologically advanced army prepares for an easy victory. Hundreds of thousands of Italians—Jewish photographer Ettore among them—march on Ethiopia seeking adventure.\n\nAs the war begins in earnest, Hirut, Aster, and the other women long to do more than care for the wounded and bury the dead. When Emperor Haile Selassie goes into exile and Ethiopia quickly loses hope, it is Hirut who offers a plan to maintain morale. She helps disguise a gentle peasant as the emperor and soon becomes his guard, inspiring other women to take up arms against the Italians. But how could she have predicted her own personal war as a prisoner of one of Italy’s most vicious officers, who will force her to pose before Ettore’s camera?\n\nWhat follows is a gorgeously crafted and unputdownable exploration of female power, with Hirut as the fierce, original, and brilliant voice at its heart. In incandescent, lyrical prose, Maaza Mengiste breathes life into complicated characters on both sides of the battle line, shaping a heartrending, indelible exploration of what it means to be a woman Unfolding in a graveyard over the course of a single night, narrated by a dazzling chorus of voices, Lincoln in the Bardo <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12211719738432656856\" style=\"display:none\">In his long-awaited first novel, American master George Saunders delivers his most original, transcendent, and moving work yet. Unfolding in a graveyard over the course of a single night, narrated by a dazzling chorus of voices, Lincoln in the Bardo is a literary experience unlike any other—for no one but Saunders could conceive it.\n\nFebruary 1862. The Civil War is less than one year old. The fighting has begun in earnest, and the nation has begun to realize it is in for a long, bloody struggle. Meanwhile, President Lincoln’s beloved eleven-year-old son, Willie, lies upstairs in the White House, gravely ill. In a matter of days, despite predictions of a recovery, Willie dies and is laid to rest in a Georgetown cemetery. “My poor boy, he was too good for this earth,” the president says at the time. “God has called him home.” Newspapers report that a grief-stricken Lincoln returned to the crypt several times alone to hold his boy’s body.\n\nFrom that seed of historical truth, George Saunders spins an unforgettable story of familial love and loss that breaks free of its realistic, historical framework into a thrilling, supernatural realm both hilarious and terrifying. Willie Lincoln finds himself in a strange purgatory, where ghosts mingle, gripe, commiserate, quarrel, and enact bizarre acts of penance. Within this transitional state—called, in the Tibetan tradition, the bardo—a monumental struggle erupts over young Willie’s soul.\n\nLincoln in the Bardo is an astonishing feat of imagination and a bold step forward from one of the most important and influential writers of his generation. Formally daring, generous in spirit, deeply concerned with matters of the heart, it is a testament to fiction’s ability to speak honestly and powerfully to the things that really matter to us. Saunders has invented a thrilling new form that deploys a kaleidoscopic, theatrical panorama of voices—living and dead, historical and invented—to ask a timeless, profound question: How do we live and Soon other bodies are discovered, in increasingly strange circumstances. As suspicions mount, Janina inserts herself into the investigation, certain that she knows whodunit. If only anyone would pay her mind... \n\nA deeply satisfying thriller cum fairy tale, Drive Your Plow over the Bones of the Dead is a provocative exploration of the murky borderland between sanity and madness, justice and tradition, autonomy and fate. Whom do we deem sane? it asks.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/56064.Marlon_James">About Marlon James</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/56064.Marlon_James"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_111x148-e08b4682eea7088f8cdfd67c131d51cd.png);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/56064.Marlon_James">Marlon James
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,447
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1230967725706494780">Marlon James is a Jamaican-born writer. He has published three novels: <i>John Crow's Devil</i> (2005), <i>The Book of Night Women</i> (2009) and <i>A Brief History of Seven Killings</i> (2014), winner of the 2015 Man Booker Prize. Now living in Minneapolis, James teaches literature at Macalester College in St. Paul, Minnesota.<br/><br/>James was born in Kingston, Jamaica, to parents who were both in the Jamaican police: his mo</span>
  <span id="freeText1230967725706494780" style="display:none">Marlon James is a Jamaican-born writer. He has published three novels: <i>John Crow's Devil</i> (2005), <i>The Book of Night Women</i> (2009) and <i>A Brief History of Seven Killings</i> (2014), winner of the 2015 Man Booker Prize. Now living in Minneapolis, James teaches literature at Macalester College in St. Paul, Minnesota.<br /><br />James was born in Kingston, Jamaica, to parents who were both in the Jamaican police: his mother (who gave him his first prose book, a collection of stories by O. Henry) became a detective and his father (from whom James took a love of Shakespeare and Coleridge) a lawyer. James is a 1991 graduate of the University of the West Indies, where he read Language and Literature. He received a master's degree in creative writing from Wilkes University (2006).<br /><br />James has taught English and creative writing at Macalester College since 2007. His first novel, <i>John Crow's Devil</i> — which was rejected 70 times before being accepted for publication — tells the story of a biblical struggle in a remote Jamaican village in 1957. His second novel, <i>The Book of Night Women</i>, is about a slave woman's revolt in a Jamaican plantation in the early 19th century. His most recent novel, 2014's <i>A Brief History of Seven Killings</i>, explores several decades of Jamaican history and political instability through the perspectives of many narrators. It won the fiction category of the 2015 OCM Bocas Prize for Caribbean Literature and the 2015 Man Booker Prize for Fiction, having been the first book by a Jamaican author ever to be shortlisted. He is the second Caribbean winner of the prize, following Trinidad-born V. S. Naipaul who won in 1971.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1230967725706494780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
